<p><b>Homs</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English" title="Help:IPA for English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="'h' in 'hi'">h</span><span title="/ɔː/ 'au' in 'fraud'">ɔː</span><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span></span>/</a></span></span>;<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span lang="ar" dir="rtl" xml:lang="ar">حمص</span>‎‎ / <a href="/wiki/ALA-LC" class="mw-redirect" title="ALA-LC">ALA-LC</a>: <i>Ḥimṣ</i>), previously known as <i>Emesa</i> (<a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Greek</a>: Ἔμεσα <i>Emesa</i>),<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> is a city in western <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a> and the capital of the <a href="/wiki/Homs_Governorate" title="Homs Governorate">Homs Governorate</a>. It is 501 metres (1,644&#160;ft) above sea level and is located 162 kilometres (101&#160;mi) north of <a href="/wiki/Damascus" title="Damascus">Damascus</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Homsonline_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Homsonline-4">[4]</a></sup> Located on the <a href="/wiki/Orontes_River" title="Orontes River">Orontes River</a>, Homs is also the central link between the interior cities and the Mediterranean coast.</p>
<p>Before the Syrian war, Homs was a major industrial centre, and with a population of at least 652,609 people in 2004,<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> it was the third largest city in Syria after <a href="/wiki/Aleppo" title="Aleppo">Aleppo</a> to the north and the capital <a href="/wiki/Damascus" title="Damascus">Damascus</a> to the south. Its population reflects Syria's general religious diversity, composed of <a href="/wiki/Sunni" class="mw-redirect" title="Sunni">Sunni</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alawite" class="mw-redirect" title="Alawite">Alawite</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Christianity" title="Eastern Christianity">Christian</a>. There are a number of historic mosques and churches in the city, and it is close to the <a href="/wiki/Krak_des_Chevaliers" title="Krak des Chevaliers">Krak des Chevaliers</a> castle, a <a href="/wiki/World_heritage_site" class="mw-redirect" title="World heritage site">world heritage site</a>.</p>
<p>Homs did not emerge into the historical record until the 1st century BCE at the time of the <a href="/wiki/Seleucid" class="mw-redirect" title="Seleucid">Seleucids</a>. It later became the capital of a kingdom ruled by the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Family_of_Emesa" class="mw-redirect" title="Royal Family of Emesa">Emesani dynasty</a> who gave the city its name. Originally a center of worship for the sun god <a href="/wiki/El-Gabal" class="mw-redirect" title="El-Gabal">El-Gabal</a>, it later gained importance in <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a> under the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantines</a>. Homs was conquered by the Muslims in the 7th century and made capital of a <a href="/wiki/Jund_Hims" title="Jund Hims">district</a> that bore its current name. Throughout the Islamic era, Muslim dynasties contending for control of Syria sought after Homs due to the city's strategic position in the area. Homs began to decline under the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottomans</a> and only in the 19th century did the city regain its economic importance when its cotton industry boomed. During <a href="/wiki/French_Mandate_of_Syria" class="mw-redirect" title="French Mandate of Syria">French Mandate</a> rule, the city became a center of insurrection and, after independence in 1946, a center of <a href="/wiki/Ba%27ath_Party" title="Ba'ath Party">Baathist</a> resistance to the first Syrian governments.</p>
<p>In the ongoing <a href="/wiki/Syrian_civil_war" class="mw-redirect" title="Syrian civil war">Syrian civil war</a>, Homs became an opposition stronghold and the Syrian government launched a military assault against the city in May 2011. The following <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Homs" title="Siege of Homs">Siege of Homs</a> left much of the city completely destroyed and thousands dead.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> The war in the city ended in 2015 when rebels evacuated the city, resulting in a government victory.</p>
<p></p>

<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-19"><a href="#Government"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">Government</span></a></li>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-20"><a href="#Education"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">Education</span></a></li>

</ul>
</li>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-24"><a href="#Twin_towns_.E2.80.93_Sister_cities"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">Twin towns – Sister cities</span></a></li>

</ul>
</div>
<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Homs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The origin of the city's modern name is that it is an <a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a> form of the city's <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> name "Emesus", derived from the <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> "Emesa" or "Emesos".<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> The name "Emesa" seems to derive from the nomadic Arab tribe, called Emesenoi by the Greeks and the Romans, that inhabited the region prior to Roman influence in the area.<sup id="cite_ref-Ball_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ball-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<p>"Emesa" was shortened to "Homs" or "Hims" by its Arab inhabitants, many of whom settled there prior to the <a href="/wiki/Muslim_conquest_of_Syria" class="mw-redirect" title="Muslim conquest of Syria">Muslim conquest of Syria</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Dumper1_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dumper1-10">[10]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> This name has been preserved throughout the period of Islamic rule continuing to the present day. It was known as "la Chamelle" by the <a href="/wiki/Crusades" title="Crusades">Crusaders</a>, although they never ruled the city.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Homs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>For approximately 2,000 years, Homs has served as a key agricultural market, production site and trade center for the villages of <a href="/wiki/Northern_Syria" class="mw-redirect" title="Northern Syria">northern Syria</a>. It has also provided security services to the hinterland of Syria, protecting it from invading forces.<sup id="cite_ref-Dumper1_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dumper1-10">[10]</a></sup> Excavations at the Citadel of Homs indicate that the earliest settlement at the site dates back to around 2300&#160;BCE. Biblical scholars have identified the city with <a href="/wiki/Zobah" title="Zobah">Zobah</a> mentioned in the <a href="/wiki/Bible" title="Bible">Bible</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Dumper1_10-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dumper1-10">[10]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Cook_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cook-14">[14]</a></sup> In 1274&#160;BCE,<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> a <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Kadesh" title="Battle of Kadesh">battle</a> took place between the forces of the <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Egyptian Empire">Egyptian Empire</a> under <a href="/wiki/Ramesses_II" title="Ramesses II">Ramesses II</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Hittite_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Hittite Empire">Hittite Empire</a> under <a href="/wiki/Muwatalli_II" title="Muwatalli II">Muwatalli II</a> at the city of <a href="/wiki/Kadesh" title="Kadesh">Kadesh</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Orontes_River" title="Orontes River">Orontes River</a> near Homs.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> It was possibly the largest <a href="/wiki/Chariot" title="Chariot">chariot</a> battle ever fought, involving perhaps 5,000–6,000 chariots.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Emesani_dynasty_and_Roman_rule">Emesani dynasty and Roman rule</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Homs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Emesani dynasty and Roman rule">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bronze-Uranius_Antoninus-Elagabal_stone-SGI_4414.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Bronze-Uranius_Antoninus-Elagabal_stone-SGI_4414.jpg/220px-Bronze-Uranius_Antoninus-Elagabal_stone-SGI_4414.jpg" width="220" height="103" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Bronze-Uranius_Antoninus-Elagabal_stone-SGI_4414.jpg/330px-Bronze-Uranius_Antoninus-Elagabal_stone-SGI_4414.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Bronze-Uranius_Antoninus-Elagabal_stone-SGI_4414.jpg/440px-Bronze-Uranius_Antoninus-Elagabal_stone-SGI_4414.jpg 2x" data-file-width="646" data-file-height="302" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bronze-Uranius_Antoninus-Elagabal_stone-SGI_4414.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Emesa temple to the sun god El-Gabal, with the holy stone, on the reverse of this bronze coin by <a href="/wiki/Roman_usurper" title="Roman usurper">Roman usurper</a> <a href="/wiki/Uranius" title="Uranius">Uranius Antoninus</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Royal_family_of_Emesa" title="Royal family of Emesa">Royal family of Emesa</a> and <a href="/wiki/Elagabalus_(deity)" title="Elagabalus (deity)">Elagabalus (deity)</a></div>
<p>Homs itself may have been founded by <a href="/wiki/Seleucus_I_Nicator" title="Seleucus I Nicator">Seleucus I Nicator</a> who established the <a href="/wiki/Seleucid_Empire" title="Seleucid Empire">Seleucid Empire</a> upon the death of <a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great" title="Alexander the Great">Alexander the Great</a>, although the city did not emerge in the light of history until the 1st century BCE. At this time, <a href="/wiki/Greeks" title="Greeks">Greek</a> philosopher <a href="/wiki/Strabo" title="Strabo">Strabo</a> spoke of a tent-dwelling tribe called the "Emesani" living in the area around the Orontes and south of the <a href="/wiki/Apamea_(Syria)" class="mw-redirect" title="Apamea (Syria)">Apamea region</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Dumper1_10-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dumper1-10">[10]</a></sup> They were an Arab tribe in the area and settled in Emesa—which derived its name from them. Upon <a href="/wiki/Pompey" title="Pompey">Pompey</a>'s incorporation of the Seleucid state of Syria into the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a> in 64&#160;BCE, the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Family_of_Emesa" class="mw-redirect" title="Royal Family of Emesa">Emesani dynasty</a> were confirmed in their rule as <a href="/wiki/Client_state" title="Client state">client kings</a> of the Romans for aiding their troops in various wars. Their chief at the time, Sampsiceramus I, gained the status as King of Emesa, but their capital was at <a href="/wiki/Arethusa_(see)" class="mw-redirect" title="Arethusa (see)">Arethusa</a>, a city north of Emesa, also along the Orontes River. At its greatest extent, the kingdom's boundaries extended from the <a href="/wiki/Bekaa_Valley" class="mw-redirect" title="Bekaa Valley">Bekaa Valley</a> in the west to the border with <a href="/wiki/Palmyra" title="Palmyra">Palmyra</a> in the east, and from <a href="/wiki/Yabrud" class="mw-redirect" title="Yabrud">Yabrud</a> in the south to <a href="/wiki/Al-Rastan" title="Al-Rastan">al-Rastan</a> (Arethusa) in the north. The kingdom of Sampsiceramus I, was the first of Rome's Arab clients on the desert fringes.<sup id="cite_ref-Ball_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ball-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Krak_des_Chevaliers_01.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Krak_des_Chevaliers_01.jpg/220px-Krak_des_Chevaliers_01.jpg" width="220" height="125" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Krak_des_Chevaliers_01.jpg/330px-Krak_des_Chevaliers_01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Krak_des_Chevaliers_01.jpg/440px-Krak_des_Chevaliers_01.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3044" data-file-height="1723" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Krak_des_Chevaliers_01.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Krak_des_Chevaliers" title="Krak des Chevaliers">Krak des Chevaliers</a>, a famous world heritage site near Homs</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The city of Emesa grew to prominence after the new-found wealth of the Emesani dynasty, governed first by one of the sons of Sampsiceramus I, <a href="/wiki/Iamblichus_(phylarch)" title="Iamblichus (phylarch)">Iamblichus I</a> who made it the kingdom's capital.<sup id="cite_ref-Ball_9-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ball-9">[9]</a></sup> The Emesani proved their loyalty to Rome once more when they aided Gaius <a href="/wiki/Julius_Caesar" title="Julius Caesar">Julius Caesar</a> in his siege of <a href="/wiki/Alexandria" title="Alexandria">Alexandria</a> in 48&#160;BCE, by sending him army detachments. Subsequently, they became embroiled in the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Civil_War" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Civil War">Roman Civil War</a> between the rebelling <a href="/wiki/Mark_Antony" title="Mark Antony">Mark Antony</a> and the pro-Caesar <a href="/wiki/Augustus" title="Augustus">Octavian</a>. Iamblichus I took the side of Octavian, and so upon encouragement from Antony, Iamblichus's brother Alexio I usurped the throne and put Iamblichus I to death in 31 BCE. Octavian's forces prevailed in the war, however, and as a result the kingdom's throne was reverted to Iamblichus II (the son of Iamblichus I) after Alexio I was executed for treason. Under the stable rule of Iamblichus II, emerged a new era of peace from 20 BCE to 14, known as Emesa's "<a href="/wiki/Golden_Age" title="Golden Age">Golden Age</a>." In 32, <a href="/wiki/Baalbek" title="Baalbek">Heliopolis</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Beqaa_Valley" title="Beqaa Valley">Beqaa Valley</a> came under the kingdom's control.<sup id="cite_ref-Ball_9-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ball-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<p>Relations with the Roman government grew closer when <a href="/wiki/Sohaemus_of_Emesa" title="Sohaemus of Emesa">King Sohaemus</a> inherited the kingship. Under him, Emesa sent the Roman military a regular levy of archers and assisted them in their <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Jerusalem_(70)" class="mw-redirect" title="Siege of Jerusalem (70)">siege of Jerusalem</a> in 70. Sohaemus had died in 73 and was succeeded by his son, Alexio II. Despite the fact that the Emesani dynasty were loyal allies to Rome, for unknown reasons the Roman state reduced the autonomous rule of the Emesani dynasty. Alexio II and his priest king successors had only ceremonial authority. It remains unclear why this occurred to the Emesani dynasty. Alexio II died in 78 and he was succeeded by his son Sampsiceramus III. The generations after Alexio II, are not recorded sufficiently to accurately present a pedigree.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> The integration of client states was actively pursued under the Flavians. Perhaps as early as 72, but certainly before 78, Sampsigeramus of Emesa was deposed and his principality incorporated into the province.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Frescos_in_Saint_Elian_Church_-_Hims,_Syria.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/ce/Frescos_in_Saint_Elian_Church_-_Hims%2C_Syria.jpg/220px-Frescos_in_Saint_Elian_Church_-_Hims%2C_Syria.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/ce/Frescos_in_Saint_Elian_Church_-_Hims%2C_Syria.jpg/330px-Frescos_in_Saint_Elian_Church_-_Hims%2C_Syria.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/ce/Frescos_in_Saint_Elian_Church_-_Hims%2C_Syria.jpg/440px-Frescos_in_Saint_Elian_Church_-_Hims%2C_Syria.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="768" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Frescos_in_Saint_Elian_Church_-_Hims,_Syria.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Some frescoes inside the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_Saint_Elian" title="Church of Saint Elian">Church of Saint Elian</a> date back to the 6th century</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Under the Romans, Emesa began to show attributes of a Greek <a href="/wiki/City-state" title="City-state">city-state</a> and traces of Roman town planning still remain. Its transformation into a major city was completed under the reign of Emperor <a href="/wiki/Antoninus_Pius" title="Antoninus Pius">Antoninus Pius</a> (138–161) when Emesa began to <a href="/wiki/Mint_(coin)#Ancient_mints" class="mw-redirect" title="Mint (coin)">mint coins</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Dumper1_10-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dumper1-10">[10]</a></sup> By the 3rd century, it grew prosperous and well integrated into the Roman Orient. This was partly due to the marriage of Emperor Lucius <a href="/wiki/Septimius_Severus" title="Septimius Severus">Septimius Severus</a> to a woman from a family of notables based in Emesa. It attained the coveted status as a Roman <i><a href="/wiki/Colonia_(Roman)" title="Colonia (Roman)">colonia</a></i> by Severus, and two other emperors of <a href="/wiki/Severan_Dynasty" class="mw-redirect" title="Severan Dynasty">Severan Dynasty</a> were raised in Emesa. One of them was <a href="/wiki/Elagabalus" title="Elagabalus">Elagabalus</a> who served as the high priest at the Temple of <a href="/wiki/Elagabalus_(deity)" title="Elagabalus (deity)">El-Gebal</a>, the local sun god. He brought the image of this god, a conical black stone, to the <a href="/wiki/Elagabalium" title="Elagabalium">Elagabalium</a> in Rome.<sup id="cite_ref-Dumper_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dumper-20">[20]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<p>Emesa also grew wealthy because it formed a link in the eastern trade funneled through <a href="/wiki/Palmyra" title="Palmyra">Palmyra</a>, however, this dependence also caused the city's downfall when Palmyra sank to insignificance in the 4th century. Nonetheless, Emesa at this time had grown to rank with the important cities of <a href="/wiki/Tyre,_Lebanon" title="Tyre, Lebanon">Tyre</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sidon" title="Sidon">Sidon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Beirut" title="Beirut">Beirut</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Damascus" title="Damascus">Damascus</a>. It also continued to retain local significance, because it was the market center for the surrounding villages. The city remained a strong center of paganism, because of the Temple of El-Gabal. After one of his victories over <a href="/wiki/Zenobia" title="Zenobia">Zenobia</a>, Emperor <a href="/wiki/Aurelian" title="Aurelian">Aurelian</a> visited the city to pay thanks to the deity.<sup id="cite_ref-Dumper_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dumper-20">[20]</a></sup></p>
<p>Due to the strength of the pagan sun cult in Emesa, Christians initially did not settle in the city. <a href="/wiki/Eusebius_of_Caesarea" class="mw-redirect" title="Eusebius of Caesarea">Eusebius</a> writes that Silvanus, the city's first bishop, had no jurisdiction over the city, but the surrounding villages. He was executed by <a href="/wiki/Julian_the_Apostate" class="mw-redirect" title="Julian the Apostate">Emperor Julian</a> and succeeded by Bishop Antonius—the first bishop to settle Emesa.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> By the 5th century, <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a> was well established under the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine Empire</a>; however, few ancient Christian inscriptions exist in Homs today.<sup id="cite_ref-Dumper_20-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dumper-20">[20]</a></sup> Under the Byzantines, the city became an important center for <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Christianity" title="Eastern Christianity">Eastern Christianity</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Carter2008-2_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carter2008-2-23">[23]</a></sup> Initially a <a href="/wiki/Diocese" title="Diocese">diocese</a>, Homs was given the status of <a href="/wiki/Ecclesiastical_metropolis" class="mw-redirect" title="Ecclesiastical metropolis">ecclesiastical metropolis</a> after the discovery of <a href="/wiki/John_the_Baptist" title="John the Baptist">John the Baptist</a>'s head in a nearby area in 452.<sup id="cite_ref-Dumper_20-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dumper-20">[20]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Arab_Caliphate">Arab Caliphate</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Homs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Arab Caliphate">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Prior to the <a href="/wiki/Muslim_conquest_of_Syria" class="mw-redirect" title="Muslim conquest of Syria">Muslim conquest of Syria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tribes_of_Arabia" title="Tribes of Arabia">Arab tribes</a>, particularly the <a href="/wiki/Banu_Kalb" title="Banu Kalb">Banu Kalb</a> settled around Emesa, ensuring its position as an important <a href="/wiki/Qays_and_Yaman_tribes" class="mw-redirect" title="Qays and Yaman tribes">Yamani</a> center. The <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine</a> emperor <a href="/wiki/Heraclius" title="Heraclius">Heraclius</a> abandoned the city—which served as his headquarters<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup>—after the his army's defeat by the <a href="/wiki/Rashidun_Caliphate" title="Rashidun Caliphate">Rashidun Muslims</a> under Caliph <a href="/wiki/Umar_ibn_al-Khattab" class="mw-redirect" title="Umar ibn al-Khattab">Umar ibn al-Khattab</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Yarmouk" title="Battle of Yarmouk">Battle of Yarmouk</a> in southern Syria. In 637 CE, the <a href="/wiki/Rashidun_army" title="Rashidun army">Rashidun army</a> led by <a href="/wiki/Khalid_ibn_al-Walid" title="Khalid ibn al-Walid">Khalid ibn al-Walid</a> captured Emesa <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Emesa" title="Siege of Emesa">peacefully</a> because its inhabitants agreed to pay a substantial ransom of 71,000 to 170,000 <a href="/wiki/Dinar" title="Dinar">dinars</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Dumper_20-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dumper-20">[20]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hamawi_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hamawi-26">[26]</a></sup> The caliph Umar established Homs as the capital of <a href="/wiki/Jund_Hims" title="Jund Hims">Jund Hims</a>, a district of the province of <a href="/wiki/Bilad_al-Sham" title="Bilad al-Sham">Bilad al-Sham</a>, encompassing the towns of <a href="/wiki/Latakia" title="Latakia">Latakia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jableh" title="Jableh">Jableh</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tartus" title="Tartus">Tartus</a> along the coast, <a href="/wiki/Palmyra" title="Palmyra">Palmyra</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Syrian_Desert" title="Syrian Desert">Syrian Desert</a> and the territory in between, including the town of <a href="/wiki/Hama" title="Hama">Hama</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> Homs was likely the first city in Syria to have a substantial <a href="/wiki/Muslim" title="Muslim">Muslim</a> population.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Muslims transformed half of St. John's Church into the city's Friday Mosque (<a href="/wiki/Great_Mosque_of_al-Nuri_(Homs)" title="Great Mosque of al-Nuri (Homs)">Great Mosque of al-Nuri</a>) and Homs soon became a centre of <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islamic</a> piety since some 500 <a href="/wiki/Sahaba" class="mw-redirect" title="Sahaba">companions</a> of <a href="/wiki/Muhammad" title="Muhammad">Muhammad</a> settled there after its conquest.<sup id="cite_ref-Dumper_20-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dumper-20">[20]</a></sup> The tombs of Khalid ibn al-Walid, his son Abd al-Rahman, and Ubaid Allah, the son of Caliph Umar are located in the city.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> During the <a href="/wiki/First_Fitna" title="First Fitna">conflict</a> between the <a href="/wiki/Umayyad" class="mw-redirect" title="Umayyad">Umayyads</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ali" title="Ali">Ali</a>, the inhabitants of Homs allied themselves with Ali and when the latter was defeated, the Umayyad caliph <a href="/wiki/Mu%27awiyah" class="mw-redirect" title="Mu'awiyah">Mu'awiyah</a> hived the northern half of Jund Hims to form a separate district, <a href="/wiki/Jund_Qinnasrin" title="Jund Qinnasrin">Jund Qinnasrin</a>, apparently as punishment.<sup id="cite_ref-Dumper_20-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dumper-20">[20]</a></sup> Ali's oratory (<i>mash-had 'Ali</i>) was located in the city, and Islamic tradition claims his fingerprints are engraved on it.<sup id="cite_ref-Hamawi_26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hamawi-26">[26]</a></sup> Despite repression by the Umayyads, Homs remained a center of <a href="/wiki/Shia_Islam" title="Shia Islam">Shia Islam</a> for a while longer. As a stronghold of the Banu Kalb, the city became involved in the latter's conflicts with the <a href="/wiki/Qais" class="mw-redirect" title="Qais">Qais</a> tribal faction. The last Umayyad caliph, <a href="/wiki/Marwan_II" title="Marwan II">Marwan II</a>, enjoyed the support of the Qais and subsequently razed the city walls in response to a rebellion by the Banu Kalb.<sup id="cite_ref-Dumper_20-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dumper-20">[20]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Interior_-_Al-Nuri_Mosque_-_Hims,_Syria.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Interior_-_Al-Nuri_Mosque_-_Hims%2C_Syria.jpg/220px-Interior_-_Al-Nuri_Mosque_-_Hims%2C_Syria.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Interior_-_Al-Nuri_Mosque_-_Hims%2C_Syria.jpg/330px-Interior_-_Al-Nuri_Mosque_-_Hims%2C_Syria.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Interior_-_Al-Nuri_Mosque_-_Hims%2C_Syria.jpg/440px-Interior_-_Al-Nuri_Mosque_-_Hims%2C_Syria.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="768" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Interior_-_Al-Nuri_Mosque_-_Hims,_Syria.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The interior of the <a href="/wiki/Great_Mosque_of_al-Nuri_(Homs)" title="Great Mosque of al-Nuri (Homs)">Great Mosque of al-Nuri</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 750 the <a href="/wiki/Abbasid_Caliphate" title="Abbasid Caliphate">Abbasids</a> wrested control of Syria, including Homs, from the Umayyads, and the Arab tribes revolted. Despite the prosperity Homs experienced during this era, Abbasid rule was generally not welcomed nevertheless. During and after the reign of caliph <a href="/wiki/Harun_al-Rashid" title="Harun al-Rashid">Harun al-Rashid</a> (796-809), the Abbasid authorities sent numerous punitive expeditions against Homs.<sup id="cite_ref-Dumper_20-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dumper-20">[20]</a></sup> Under the reign of <a href="/wiki/Al-Mutawakkil" title="Al-Mutawakkil">al-Mutawakkil</a>, in October 855, the Christian population revolted in response to <a href="/wiki/Jizya" title="Jizya">additional taxation</a>. The caliph put down the revolt by expelling Christians from the city, burning down their churches and executing members of their leadership.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<p>With Abbasid rule over the Caliphate weakening in the mid-9th century, Homs became sought after by rebel dynasties contending for control of Syria due to the city's strategic position. Initially, the <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>-based <a href="/wiki/Tulunids" title="Tulunids">Tulunids</a> came into control of it, but they were forced out by the <a href="/wiki/Aleppo" title="Aleppo">Aleppo</a>-based <a href="/wiki/Hamdanid" class="mw-redirect" title="Hamdanid">Hamdanids</a> who were briefly succeeded by the <a href="/wiki/Qarmatians" title="Qarmatians">Qarmatians</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Dumper_20-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dumper-20">[20]</a></sup> after their Turkish rebel ally <a href="/wiki/Aftakin" title="Aftakin">Aftakin</a> invaded northern Syria and established Homs as his base.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> In 891 Muslim geographer <a href="/wiki/Al-Yaqubi" class="mw-redirect" title="Al-Yaqubi">al-Yaqubi</a> noted that Homs was situated along a broad river which served as a source of drinking water for the inhabitants.<sup id="cite_ref-le_Strange2_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-le_Strange2-32">[32]</a></sup> It was one of the largest cities in Syria and had several smaller districts surrounding it. In 944 the Hamdanids took definitive control of the city, dominating it until 1016.<sup id="cite_ref-Dumper_20-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dumper-20">[20]</a></sup> Arab geographer <a href="/wiki/Al-Mas%27udi" class="mw-redirect" title="Al-Mas'udi">al-Mas'udi</a> claimed in the early 10th century that Homs was "noted for the personal beauty of its inhabitants."<sup id="cite_ref-le_Strange2_32-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-le_Strange2-32">[32]</a></sup> In 985 <a href="/wiki/Al-Muqaddasi" title="Al-Muqaddasi">al-Muqaddasi</a> noted that Homs was the largest city in all of Syria, but it had suffered "great misfortunes" and was "threatened with ruin." He stated that when the city was conquered by the Muslims they turned half of its church into a mosque.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup></p>
<p>For around thirty years during the 10th century, Homs was raided by the Byzantines and its inhabitants were subject to slaughter and plunder while the city's mosque was briefly restored as a church. Throughout most of the 11th century, the Byzantine raids receded greatly and the <a href="/wiki/Mirdasid" class="mw-redirect" title="Mirdasid">Mirdasids</a> of the Banu Kalb tribe ruled over Homs, replacing the Hamdanids. Inclined towards Shia Islam, they did not oppose the Shia <a href="/wiki/Fatimid_Caliphate" title="Fatimid Caliphate">Fatimid Caliphate</a> of Egypt which was aiming to extend its rule into northern Syria and <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a> at the time. This precipitated a <a href="/wiki/Sunni_Muslim" class="mw-redirect" title="Sunni Muslim">Sunni Muslim</a> reaction led by the <a href="/wiki/Seljuk_Turks" class="mw-redirect" title="Seljuk Turks">Seljuk Turks</a> who occupied Homs under the leadership of <a href="/wiki/Aq_Sunqur_al-Hajib" title="Aq Sunqur al-Hajib">Aq Sunqur al-Hajib</a> in 1090.<sup id="cite_ref-Dumper_20-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dumper-20">[20]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Seljuk.2C_Ayyubid.2C_and_Mamluk_rule">Seljuk, Ayyubid, and Mamluk rule</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Homs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Seljuk, Ayyubid, and Mamluk rule">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:18th_century_original_drawing_of_the_castle_of_Hims_by_Cassas.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/18th_century_original_drawing_of_the_castle_of_Hims_by_Cassas.jpg/220px-18th_century_original_drawing_of_the_castle_of_Hims_by_Cassas.jpg" width="220" height="159" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/18th_century_original_drawing_of_the_castle_of_Hims_by_Cassas.jpg/330px-18th_century_original_drawing_of_the_castle_of_Hims_by_Cassas.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/18th_century_original_drawing_of_the_castle_of_Hims_by_Cassas.jpg/440px-18th_century_original_drawing_of_the_castle_of_Hims_by_Cassas.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="739" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:18th_century_original_drawing_of_the_castle_of_Hims_by_Cassas.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Romantic illustration of Homs by <a href="/wiki/Louis-Fran%C3%A7ois_Cassas" title="Louis-François Cassas">Louis-François Cassas</a>. The artist in the foreground is shown sketching the Citadel of Homs, surrounded by his guards and the inquisitive locals</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/First_Crusade" title="First Crusade">First Crusade</a> was launched in 1096, and in 1098, the Crusaders captured <a href="/wiki/Antioch" title="Antioch">Antioch</a> to the northwest, looted <a href="/wiki/Ma%27arat_al-Numan" class="mw-redirect" title="Ma'arat al-Numan">Ma'arat al-Numan</a>, and finally besieged Homs itself. Although they managed to cut the city off from its main port <a href="/wiki/Tartus" title="Tartus">Tartus</a>, they failed in taking the city. Soon after, Homs came under the control of the Seljuk ruler of <a href="/wiki/Damascus" title="Damascus">Damascus</a> who transformed it into a large, fortified camp and key fortress effectively preventing the Crusaders from penetrating deeper into Muslim territory. Immune from attack, Homs became a point where the Muslims could marshal their forces and launch raids against Crusader holdings along the Mediterranean coast.<sup id="cite_ref-Dumper_20-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dumper-20">[20]</a></sup> In the early 12th century, the Seljuks engaged in internal fighting, during which Homs was often a prize. In 1149 the <a href="/wiki/Mosul" title="Mosul">Mosul</a>-based <a href="/wiki/Zengid" class="mw-redirect" title="Zengid">Zengids</a> under <a href="/wiki/Nur_ad-Din_Zangi" class="mw-redirect" title="Nur ad-Din Zangi">Nur al-Din</a> captured the city.<sup id="cite_ref-Dumper2_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dumper2-34">[34]</a></sup></p>
<p>Muslim geographer <a href="/wiki/Al-Idrisi" class="mw-redirect" title="Al-Idrisi">Al-Idrisi</a> noted in 1154 that Homs was populous, had paved streets, possessed one of the largest mosques in Syria, contained open markets, and was frequented by travelers attracted to its "products and rarities of all kinds." He also reported that its residents were "pleasant; living with them is easy, and their manners are agreeable. The women are beautiful and are celebrated for their fine skin."<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> A series of earthquakes in 1157 inflicted heavy damage upon Homs and its fortress, then in 1170, a minor quake finished off the latter. However, because of its strategic importance, being opposite of the Crusader <a href="/wiki/County_of_Tripoli" title="County of Tripoli">County of Tripoli</a>, the city and its fortifications were soon restored. In 1164 Nur al-Din awarded Homs to <a href="/wiki/Shirkuh" title="Shirkuh">Asad ad-Din Shirkuh</a> as a fief, but reclaimed it five years later following Shirkuh's death. The latter's nephew, <a href="/wiki/Saladin" title="Saladin">Saladin</a>, gained control of the city in 1175 and in 1179, after reorganizing his territories in northern Syria, restored the fief to his <a href="/wiki/Ayyubid_dynasty" title="Ayyubid dynasty">Ayyubid dynasty</a>. Shirkuh's descendants retained Homs for nearly a century until 1262 with the death of <a href="/wiki/Al-Ashraf_Musa,_Emir_of_Homs" title="Al-Ashraf Musa, Emir of Homs">al-Ashraf Musa</a>. In 1225 Arab geographer <a href="/wiki/Yaqut_al-Hamawi" title="Yaqut al-Hamawi">Yaqut al-Hamawi</a> mentioned that Homs was large, celebrated and walled, having a strongly fortified castle on its southern hill.<sup id="cite_ref-Hamawi_26-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hamawi-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<p>Towards the end of Ayyubid rule, Homs remained a centrepiece of the wars between them and the Crusaders, as well as internecine conflicts with the <a href="/wiki/Mongol_Empire" title="Mongol Empire">Mongol Empire</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Mamluk" title="Mamluk">Mamluks</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Dumper2_34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dumper2-34">[34]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/First_Battle_of_Homs" title="First Battle of Homs">first battle</a> between the Mongols and the Mamluks took place on December 10, 1260, ending in a decisive Mamluk victory. A <a href="/wiki/Second_Battle_of_Homs" title="Second Battle of Homs">second battle</a> was fought on October 29, 1281, also ending in a Mamluk victory. The Mamluks were finally defeated in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Wadi_al-Khazandar" title="Battle of Wadi al-Khazandar">Battle of Wadi al-Khazandar</a>, also known as the "Third Battle of Homs," in 1299.<sup id="cite_ref-Dumper2_34-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dumper2-34">[34]</a></sup></p>
<p>Homs declined politically after falling to the Mamluks under <a href="/wiki/Baibars" title="Baibars">Baibars</a> because their campaigns effectively drove out the Crusaders and the Mongols from the entirety of Syria. At the beginning of the 14th century, the city was merely the capital of the smallest province of Syria and was often attached to the province of Damascus.<sup id="cite_ref-Dumper2_34-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dumper2-34">[34]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Batuta" class="mw-redirect" title="Ibn Batuta">Ibn Batuta</a> visited Homs in 1355, writing that it had fine trees, good markets, and a "fine Friday Mosque," noting that all of its inhabitants were Arabs.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Timur" title="Timur">Timur</a> seized the city in 1400, and later in the 15th century as Mamluk weakness had brought insecurity to the countryside, Homs was ravaged by <a href="/wiki/Bedouin" title="Bedouin">Bedouin</a> raids; In 1510 a powerful tribe led by al-Fadl bin Nu'ayr was sent on an expedition by the governor of Damascus to loot the city markets as Homs had failed to pay compensation for his "services."<sup id="cite_ref-Dumper2_34-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dumper2-34">[34]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ottoman_rule">Ottoman rule</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Homs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Ottoman rule">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Khaled_Ebn_El-Walid_Mosque3.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Khaled_Ebn_El-Walid_Mosque3.jpg/220px-Khaled_Ebn_El-Walid_Mosque3.jpg" width="220" height="119" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Khaled_Ebn_El-Walid_Mosque3.jpg/330px-Khaled_Ebn_El-Walid_Mosque3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Khaled_Ebn_El-Walid_Mosque3.jpg/440px-Khaled_Ebn_El-Walid_Mosque3.jpg 2x" data-file-width="943" data-file-height="510" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Khaled_Ebn_El-Walid_Mosque3.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Khalid_ibn_al-Walid_Mosque" title="Khalid ibn al-Walid Mosque">Khalid ibn al-Walid Mosque</a>, an example of <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_architecture" title="Ottoman architecture">Ottoman architecture</a> in Homs</div>
</div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Syria" title="Ottoman Syria">Ottoman Syria</a></div>
<p>In 1516 Homs was incorporated into the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a> and consequently suffered a greater political eclipse, but it continued to thrive as an economic center, processing the agricultural and pastoral products that flowed to it from surrounding districts.<sup id="cite_ref-Collelo1_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Collelo1-37">[37]</a></sup> Homs was particularly well known for silk and wool weaving, especially the <i>alaja</i>, which was mottled <a href="/wiki/Muslin" title="Muslin">muslin</a> run through with gold threads and used in feminine apparel. This silk was exported as far as the Ottoman capital <a href="/wiki/Istanbul" title="Istanbul">Istanbul</a>. In addition to weaving industries, there were <a href="/wiki/Olive_oil" title="Olive oil">olive oil</a> presses and water mills for wheat and sesame, while grapes and rice, grown in the surrounding marshlands from the 16th century, were found in abundance in the city's markets. Moreover, the markets of Homs were the center of a trade in livestock, where flocks of sheep and goats coming from Aleppo met camels and cattle moving north from Damascus.<sup id="cite_ref-Dumper2_34-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dumper2-34">[34]</a></sup></p>
<p>The coming of the Ottomans brought administrative changes to Homs, as it became the capital city of <i><a href="/wiki/Sanjak" title="Sanjak">sanjak</a></i> ("district") of Homs, attached to the <i><a href="/wiki/Eyalet" title="Eyalet">eyalet</a></i> ("province") of <a href="/wiki/Tripoli_Eyalet" title="Tripoli Eyalet">Tripoli</a>—its old rival.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> At this time, a <a href="/wiki/French_people" title="French people">French</a> visitor noted that the city walls and citadel were in good repair, but all within was in decay and only its covered markets "retained their beauty." In 1785 French traveler, <a href="/wiki/Constantin-Fran%C3%A7ois_Chasseb%C5%93uf" class="mw-redirect" title="Constantin-François Chassebœuf">Volney</a> wrote of the city's once great importance and its current "miserable" condition. He described it as a large, but ruined village administratively dependent on Damascus. The Ottomans did little to revitalize Homs or ensure its security against Bedouin raids. Tribal unrest throughout the 17th and 18th centuries resulted in the sacking of its markets on several occasions. Security was even more hampered, when in the 18th century, the Ottomans tore down the gates of the city's walls.<sup id="cite_ref-Dumper2_34-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dumper2-34">[34]</a></sup></p>
<p>The countryside of Homs saw an increase in Bedouin raids in the first half of the 19th century, interrupted by its occupation by <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_Ali_Dynasty" class="mw-redirect" title="Muhammad Ali Dynasty">Muhammad Ali's Egypt</a> led by <a href="/wiki/Ibrahim_Pasha_of_Egypt" title="Ibrahim Pasha of Egypt">Ibrahim Pasha</a> between 1832 and 1840.<sup id="cite_ref-Collelo1_37-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Collelo1-37">[37]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> The city rebelled against Egyptian rule and consequently, the citadel was destroyed when the Egyptians suppressed the revolt. Ottoman rule was soon restored and up to the 1860s, Homs was large enough to form a discrete economic unit of trade and processing of agricultural products from its satellite villages and the neighboring Bedouin tribes.<sup id="cite_ref-Dumper2_34-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dumper2-34">[34]</a></sup></p>
<p>The local economy was stimulated when the Ottoman government extended security to the city and its surrounding areas; new villages were established and old ones were resettled. However, Homs found itself faced with European economic competition since Ottoman rule was restored. Homs' economic importance was boosted again during the depression of the 1870s, as its cotton industry boomed due to a decline European textile production. The quality and design of cotton goods from Homs satisfied both the lower and upper classes of the local, Ottoman, and foreign markets. There were around 5,000 looms in Homs and nearby <a href="/wiki/Hama" title="Hama">Hama</a>, and one British consul referred to Homs as the "<a href="/wiki/Manchester" title="Manchester">Manchester</a> of Syria."<sup id="cite_ref-Dumper2_34-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dumper2-34">[34]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_era">Modern era</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Homs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Modern era">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Throughout the 20th century Homs held high political importance in the country and was home to several heads of state and other high-ranking government officials.<sup id="cite_ref-Dumper2_34-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dumper2-34">[34]</a></sup> During the <a href="/wiki/French_mandate_of_Syria" class="mw-redirect" title="French mandate of Syria">French mandate</a>, Homs was part of the <a href="/wiki/State_of_Damascus" title="State of Damascus">State of Damascus</a>. In Autumn 1925, the city joined Damascus and the southern <a href="/wiki/Druze" title="Druze">Druze</a> chieftains in a full-blown <a href="/wiki/Great_Syrian_Revolt" title="Great Syrian Revolt">revolt</a> against French rule.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> In 1932 the French moved their <a href="/wiki/Military_academy" title="Military academy">military academy</a> from Damascus to Homs, and it remained the only military academy in Syria until 1967.<sup id="cite_ref-commins_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-commins-41">[41]</a></sup> The French authorities had created a unit of Special Forces in which the <a href="/wiki/Alawite" class="mw-redirect" title="Alawite">Alawites</a> were given privileged positions. The military academy in Homs served as a stronghold for these Special Forces.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> The Homs Military Academy played a major role in the years following Syria's independence, as many of its graduates went on to become high-ranking officers in the <a href="/wiki/Syrian_Army" title="Syrian Army">Syrian Army</a>, many of them taking part in the series of <a href="/wiki/Coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="Coup d'état">coup d'états</a> that were to follow. An important example was <a href="/wiki/Hafez_al-Assad" title="Hafez al-Assad">Hafez al-Assad</a> who became the president of Syria from 1971 until his death in 2000.<sup id="cite_ref-Seale_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Seale-43">[43]</a></sup></p>
<p>An oil pipeline between Tripoli and <a href="/wiki/Kirkuk" title="Kirkuk">Kirkuk</a> was built in Homs in the early 1930s and it followed an ancient caravan route between Palmyra and the Mediterranean. In 1959, an oil refinery was built to process some of this oil for domestic consumption.<sup id="cite_ref-Dumper2_34-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dumper2-34">[34]</a></sup> The city's oil refinery was bombed by the <a href="/wiki/Israeli_Air_Force" title="Israeli Air Force">Israeli Air Force</a> (IAF) during the 1973 <a href="/wiki/Yom_Kippur_War" title="Yom Kippur War">Yom Kippur War</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Seale_43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Seale-43">[43]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Syrian_civil_war">Syrian civil war</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Homs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Syrian civil war">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Homs" title="Siege of Homs">Siege of Homs</a> and <a href="/wiki/2012_Homs_offensive" title="2012 Homs offensive">2012 Homs offensive</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">

<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Destruction_in_Homs_(4).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Destruction_in_Homs_%284%29.jpg/220px-Destruction_in_Homs_%284%29.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Destruction_in_Homs_%284%29.jpg/330px-Destruction_in_Homs_%284%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Destruction_in_Homs_%284%29.jpg/440px-Destruction_in_Homs_%284%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4000" data-file-height="3000" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Destruction_in_Homs_(4).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Today, large parts of Homs are destroyed or severely damaged</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Homs is one of many cities in Syria where large anti-government demonstrations have been held, part of the wider <a href="/wiki/Syrian_civil_war" class="mw-redirect" title="Syrian civil war">Syrian civil war</a>. It has been referred to as the "Capital of the revolution." Thousands to tens of thousands gathered in the city's <a href="/wiki/Quwatli_Street_(Homs)" title="Quwatli Street (Homs)">main square</a> on 17–18 April 2011 in a sit-in protesting the government of current <a href="/wiki/President_of_Syria" title="President of Syria">president</a> <a href="/wiki/Bashar_al-Assad" title="Bashar al-Assad">Bashar al-Assad</a>. At least 62 people were killed by government security forces in armed clashes against local anti-government militants.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<p>Starting on 6 May 2011, the city has been under <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Homs" title="Siege of Homs">siege</a> by the Syrian Army and security forces. The Syrian government claims it is targeting "armed gangs" and "terrorists" in the area. According to the Syrian opposition, Homs has since become a "blighted city," where authorities regularly block deliveries of medicine, food and fuel to the inhabitants of certain districts. By June, there were near-daily confrontations between protesting residents and Syrian forces. As a result of these circumstances, there have been more deaths in Homs and its vicinity than in other areas of Syria. Homs was the first Syrian city where images of al-Assad and his family were routinely torn down or defaced and the first place where Syrian forces used artillery during the uprising. The Center for Documenting Violations in Syria claims that at least 1,770 people have been killed in Homs since the uprising began.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup></p>
<p>In February 2012, Syrian Armed Forces carried out a major attack on Homs to regain control over the city which was turned into an operation center for the <a href="/wiki/Free_Syrian_Army" title="Free Syrian Army">Free Syrian Army</a>, a collection of anti-government fighters and army defectors. Ten days of operations resulted in the deaths of about 700 people in the city according to the Local Coordination Committees.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> On 1 March 2012, the Syrian Army had gained control over the <a href="/wiki/Baba_Amr" title="Baba Amr">Baba Amr</a> district while lesser clashes continued in other neighborhoods.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 9 December 2015, under a UN-negotiated deal, the remnants of anti-government forces and their families, that had been under siege the al-Wair district for three years, began to evacuate from the city.<sup id="cite_ref-evac_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-evac-48">[48]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Homs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Geography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Homs_Governorate" title="Homs Governorate">Governorate of Homs</a> is the largest in Syria. Homs, the governorate's capital, is located in central western Syria, situated along the east bank of the <a href="/wiki/Orontes_River" title="Orontes River">Orontes River</a> in a particularly fertile area. The city is in between the southern outliers of the <a href="/wiki/Syrian_Coastal_Mountain_Range" title="Syrian Coastal Mountain Range">Coastal Mountain Range</a> located to the west and <a href="/wiki/Mount_Lebanon" title="Mount Lebanon">Mount Lebanon</a>, overlooking the <a href="/wiki/Homs_Gap" title="Homs Gap">Homs Gap</a>. Because of the gap, the area around Homs receives much more rainfall than interior regions to its north and south.<sup id="cite_ref-commins_41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-commins-41">[41]</a></sup> To the east of Homs, is the <a href="/wiki/Syrian_Desert" title="Syrian Desert">Syrian Desert</a>. <a href="/wiki/Lake_Homs" title="Lake Homs">Lake Homs</a>, impounded by a <a href="/wiki/Lake_of_Homs_Dam" class="mw-redirect" title="Lake of Homs Dam">huge dam of Roman origins</a>, is to the southwest, lying some 125 kilometers (78&#160;mi) south of <a href="/wiki/Aleppo" title="Aleppo">Aleppo</a> and 34 kilometers (21&#160;mi) south of <a href="/wiki/Hama" title="Hama">Hama</a>, halfway on the road between the capital Damascus and Aleppo.<sup id="cite_ref-Dumper1_10-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dumper1-10">[10]</a></sup> The Orontes River splits the city into two main sections: To the east, on a flat land lies the city center and the main neighborhoods; to the west, lies the more recent and modern suburb of <a href="/w/index.php?title=Al-Waer&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Al-Waer (page does not exist)">al-Waer</a>. The city spans an area of 4,800 hectares (19&#160;sq&#160;mi).<sup id="cite_ref-CityCouncel_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CityCouncel-49">[49]</a></sup></p>
<p>Homs is located 162 kilometres (101&#160;mi) north of <a href="/wiki/Damascus" title="Damascus">Damascus</a>, 193 kilometres (120&#160;mi) south of <a href="/wiki/Aleppo" title="Aleppo">Aleppo</a>, 47 kilometres (29&#160;mi) south of <a href="/wiki/Hama" title="Hama">Hama</a>, and 186 kilometres (116&#160;mi) southeast of <a href="/wiki/Latakia" title="Latakia">Latakia</a> on the Mediterranean coast.<sup id="cite_ref-Homsonline_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Homsonline-4">[4]</a></sup> Nearby towns and villages include <a href="/wiki/Al-Rayyan,_Syria" title="Al-Rayyan, Syria">al-Rayyan</a> to the southeast, <a href="/wiki/Maskanah,_Homs_Governorate" title="Maskanah, Homs Governorate">Maskanah</a>, <a href="/wiki/Al-Nuqayrah" class="mw-redirect" title="Al-Nuqayrah">al-Nuqayrah</a>, <a href="/wiki/Abil" title="Abil">Abil</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kafr_Aya" title="Kafr Aya">Kafr Aya</a> to the south, <a href="/wiki/Al-Qusayr,_Syria" title="Al-Qusayr, Syria">al-Qusayr</a>, <a href="/wiki/Qattinah" title="Qattinah">Qattinah</a> and <a href="/wiki/Al-Buwaydah_al-Sharqiyah" title="Al-Buwaydah al-Sharqiyah">al-Buwaydah al-Sharqiyah</a> to the southwest, <a href="/wiki/Khirbet_Tin_Nur" title="Khirbet Tin Nur">Khirbet Tin Nur</a> to the west, <a href="/wiki/Al-Dar_al-Kabirah" title="Al-Dar al-Kabirah">al-Dar al-Kabirah</a> to the northwest, <a href="/wiki/Al-Ghantu" title="Al-Ghantu">al-Ghantu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Teir_Maalah" title="Teir Maalah">Teir Maalah</a>, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Al-Mukhtariyah&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Al-Mukhtariyah (page does not exist)">al-Mukhtariyah</a> and <a href="/wiki/Talbiseh" title="Talbiseh">Talbiseh</a> to the north, <a href="/wiki/Al-Mishirfeh" class="mw-redirect" title="Al-Mishirfeh">al-Mishirfeh</a> to the northeast and <a href="/wiki/Fairouzeh" title="Fairouzeh">Fairouzeh</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zaidal" title="Zaidal">Zaidal</a> to the east.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Old_City_and_subdivisions">Old City and subdivisions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Homs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Old City and subdivisions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Gates_of_Homs" title="Gates of Homs">Gates of Homs</a></div>
<p>The Old City is the most condensed area of Homs, and it includes the neighborhoods of Bab Tadmur, Bab al-Dreib, Bab Hud and the immediate vicinity of the citadel, covering an area of 1.2 square kilometres (0.46&#160;sq&#160;mi).<sup id="cite_ref-CityCouncel_49-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CityCouncel-49">[49]</a></sup> Little remains of the Old City; its walls and gates were demolished in the Ottoman era, but a short section of fortified wall with a circular corner tower still exists. Half a kilometer to the south, a large earth mound marks the site where the citadel once stood.<sup id="cite_ref-Carter1_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carter1-50">[50]</a></sup> To the north of the citadel lies the Christian Quarter, known as "al-Hamidiyah".<sup id="cite_ref-div_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-div-51">[51]</a></sup> This neighborhood is one of the few areas of Homs that retains its older look, with most of the <a href="/wiki/Ablaq" title="Ablaq">alternating black-and-white</a> stone buildings dating from the Mamluk era. They are still used as shops and dwellings, and there has been recent renovation.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup></p>
<p>At the time of the Abbasids, Homs was known for its seven gates. They were Bab al-Souq (Gate of the Market), Bab Tadmur (Gate of <a href="/wiki/Palmyra" title="Palmyra">Palmyra</a>), Bab al-Dreib (or Bab al-Deir), Bab al-Sebaa (Gate of the Lions), Bab al-Turkman (Gate of the <a href="/wiki/Turkmen_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Turkmen people">Turkmen</a>), Bab al-Masdoud (Closed Door), and Bab Hud (The Gate of <a href="/wiki/Hud_(prophet)" title="Hud (prophet)">Hud</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-Dumper2_34-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dumper2-34">[34]</a></sup> Only two gates—Bab Tadmor and Bab al-Dreib—remain today.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> The oldest of Homs' mosques and churches are located in the Old City.<sup id="cite_ref-Carter1_50-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carter1-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
<p>Homs consists of several subdivisions outside the Old City. The large neighbourhood of Khaldiyah spreads along its northern edge, while the more modern neighbourhoods of al-Sabil, al-Zahra, and Jub al-Jandali are situated to the east of the Old City. South of it are the neighborhoods of Bab al-Sebaa, al-Mreijeh, al-Nezha, Akrama and beyond them lay the Karm al-Zaytoun and Karm al-Loz neighbourhoods.<sup id="cite_ref-div_51-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-div-51">[51]</a></sup> The modern commercial centre lies to the west in the neighbourhood of Jouret al-Shayyah, and further west are the upscale neighbourhoods of Qusoor, al-Mahatta and al-Ghouta. The suburb of al-Waer is located even further west, separated from the city by areas of farmland called al-Basatin and the Orontes River forming a <a href="/wiki/Green_belt" title="Green belt">green belt</a> where it is forbidden to build anything.<sup id="cite_ref-div_51-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-div-51">[51]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Al-Baath_University" title="Al-Baath University">Baath University</a> complex and dormitories are located on the western-southern edge of the city next to the neighborhood of Akrama.<sup id="cite_ref-div_51-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-div-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate">Climate</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Homs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Climate">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Homs' location ensures that it receives softening influences and breezes from the Mediterranean. As a result, the city has a much milder climate than nearby Hama, with higher average rainfall of 18 inches (460&#160;mm) instead of 14 inches (360&#160;mm), but it also experiences greater winds.<sup id="cite_ref-Dumper1_10-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dumper1-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
